16 <title>

17Reasons for cannabidiol use: a cross-sectional study of CBD users, focusing on self-perceived stress, anxiety, and sleep problems | Journal of Cannabis Research | Full Text</title>

18

19

20

21

22 <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Public and medical interest in cannabidiol (CBD) has been rising, and CBD is now available from various sources. Research into the effects of low-dose CBD on outcomes like stress, anxiety, and sleep problems have been scarce, so we conducted an online survey of CBD users to better understand patterns of use, dose, and self-perceived effects of CBD. The sample consisted of 387 current or past-CBD users who answered a 20-question online survey. The survey was sent out to CBD users through email databases and social media. Participants reported basic demographics, CBD use patterns, reasons for use, and effects on anxiety, sleep, and stress. The sample (N = 387) consisted of 61.2% females, mostly between 25 and 54&#8201;years old (72.2%) and primarily based in the UK (77.4%). The top 4 reasons for using CBD were self-perceived anxiety (42.6%), sleep problems (42.5%), stress (37%), and general health and wellbeing (37%). Fifty-four per cent reported using less than 50&#8201;mg CBD daily, and 72.6% used CBD sublingually. Adjusted logistic models show females had lower odds than males of using CBD for general health and wellbeing [OR 0.45, 95% CI 0.30&#8211;0.72] and post-workout muscle-soreness [OR 0.46, 95%CI 0.24&#8211;0.91] but had higher odds of using CBD for self-perceived anxiety [OR 1.60, 95% CI 0.02&#8211;2.49] and insomnia [OR 1.87, 95% CI 1.13&#8211;3.11]. Older individuals had lower odds of using CBD for general health and wellbeing, stress, post-workout sore muscles, anxiety, skin conditions, focusing, and sleep but had higher odds of using CBD for pain. Respondents reported that CBD use was effective for stress, sleep problems, and anxiety in those who used the drug for those conditions. This survey indicated that CBD users take the drug to manage self-perceived anxiety, stress, sleep, and other symptoms, often in low doses, and these patterns vary by demographic characteristics. Further research is required to understand how low doses, representative of the general user, might impact mental health symptoms like stress, anxiety, and sleep problems."/>

785 <meta property="og:title" content="Reasons for cannabidiol use: a cross-sectional study of CBD users, focusing on self-perceived stress, anxiety, and sleep problems - Journal of Cannabis Research"/>

786

787 <meta property="og:description" content="Background Public and medical interest in cannabidiol (CBD) has been rising, and CBD is now available from various sources. Research into the effects of low-dose CBD on outcomes like stress, anxiety, and sleep problems have been scarce, so we conducted an online survey of CBD users to better understand patterns of use, dose, and self-perceived effects of CBD. Methods The sample consisted of 387 current or past-CBD users who answered a 20-question online survey. The survey was sent out to CBD users through email databases and social media. Participants reported basic demographics, CBD use patterns, reasons for use, and effects on anxiety, sleep, and stress. Results The sample (N &#x3D; 387) consisted of 61.2% females, mostly between 25 and 54years old (72.2%) and primarily based in the UK (77.4%). The top 4 reasons for using CBD were self-perceived anxiety (42.6%), sleep problems (42.5%), stress (37%), and general health and wellbeing (37%). Fifty-four per cent reported using less than 50mg CBD daily, and 72.6% used CBD sublingually. Adjusted logistic models show females had lower odds than males of using CBD for general health and wellbeing [OR 0.45, 95% CI 0.300.72] and post-workout muscle-soreness [OR 0.46, 95%CI 0.240.91] but had higher odds of using CBD for self-perceived anxiety [OR 1.60, 95% CI 0.022.49] and insomnia [OR 1.87, 95% CI 1.133.11]. Older individuals had lower odds of using CBD for general health and wellbeing, stress, post-workout sore muscles, anxiety, skin conditions, focusing, and sleep but had higher odds of using CBD for pain. Respondents reported that CBD use was effective for stress, sleep problems, and anxiety in those who used the drug for those conditions. Conclusion This survey indicated that CBD users take the drug to manage self-perceived anxiety, stress, sleep, and other symptoms, often in low doses, and these patterns vary by demographic characteristics. Further research is required to understand how low doses, representative of the general user, might impact mental health symptoms like stress, anxiety, and sleep problems."/>

1455Reasons for cannabidiol use: a cross-sectional study of CBD users, focusing on self-perceived stress, anxiety, and sleep problems</h1>

1456

1457 <ul class="c-article-author-list js-etal-collapsed" data-etal="25" data-etal-small="3" data-test="authors-list" data-component-authors-activator="authors-list">

1569

1570 <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en">

1571<div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section">

1572<h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">

1573Abstract</h2>

1574<div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content">

1575<h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">

1576Background</h3>

1577<p>

1578Public and medical interest in cannabidiol (CBD) has been rising, and CBD is now available from various sources. Research into the effects of low-dose CBD on outcomes like stress, anxiety, and sleep problems have been scarce, so we conducted an online survey of CBD users to better understand patterns of use, dose, and self-perceived effects of CBD.</p>

1579<h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">

1580Methods</h3>

1581<p>

1582The sample consisted of 387 current or past-CBD users who answered a 20-question online survey. The survey was sent out to CBD users through email databases and social media. Participants reported basic demographics, CBD use patterns, reasons for use, and effects on anxiety, sleep, and stress.</p>

1583<h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">

1584Results</h3>

1585<p>

1586The sample (<i>

1587N</i>

1588 = 387) consisted of 61.2% females, mostly between 25 and 54years old (72.2%) and primarily based in the UK (77.4%). The top 4 reasons for using CBD were self-perceived anxiety (42.6%), sleep problems (42.5%), stress (37%), and general health and wellbeing (37%). Fifty-four per cent reported using less than 50mg CBD daily, and 72.6% used CBD sublingually. Adjusted logistic models show females had lower odds than males of using CBD for general health and wellbeing [OR 0.45, 95% CI 0.300.72] and post-workout muscle-soreness [OR 0.46, 95%CI 0.240.91] but had higher odds of using CBD for self-perceived anxiety [OR 1.60, 95% CI 0.022.49] and insomnia [OR 1.87, 95% CI 1.133.11]. Older individuals had lower odds of using CBD for general health and wellbeing, stress, post-workout sore muscles, anxiety, skin conditions, focusing, and sleep but had higher odds of using CBD for pain. Respondents reported that CBD use was effective for stress, sleep problems, and anxiety in those who used the drug for those conditions.</p>

1589<h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">

1590Conclusion</h3>

1591<p>

1592This survey indicated that CBD users take the drug to manage self-perceived anxiety, stress, sleep, and other symptoms, often in low doses, and these patterns vary by demographic characteristics. Further research is required to understand how low doses, representative of the general user, might impact mental health symptoms like stress, anxiety, and sleep problems.</p>

1593</div>

1594</div>

1595</section>

1596

1597

1598

1599

1600

1601 <section data-title="Introduction">

1602<div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section">

1603<h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">

1604Introduction</h2>

1605<div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content">

1606<p>

1607In the past years, cannabidiol (CBD), one amongst hundreds of naturally occurring phytocannabinoids found in the <i>

1608Cannabis sativa</i>

1609 plant, has received a lot of attention from scientific communities, politicians, and mainstream media channels. CBD is the second most abundant cannabinoid in the <i>

1610Cannabis sativa</i>

1611 plant after delta-9-tetrahydrocannabinol (THC), but unlike THC, CBD is not intoxicating (Pertwee <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2008" title="Pertwee RG. The diverse CB 1 and CB 2 receptor pharmacology of three plant cannabinoids: 9-tetrahydrocannabinol, cannabidiol and 9-tetrahydrocannabivarin. Br J Pharmacol. 2008;153:199215." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e532">

16122008</a>

1613). In many countries, including the UK, there is unsanctioned availability of products containing CBD, from oils and capsules to chewing gums, mints, soft drinks, gummies, and intimate lubrication gels.</p>

1614<p>

1615CBD has not demonstrated any potential for abuse or dependency and is considered well tolerated with a good safety profile, according to a report released by the World Health Organization (WHO) (Geneva CANNABIDIOL (CBD) <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference n.d." title="Geneva CANNABIDIOL (CBD) . n.d. Critical Review Report Expert Committee on Drug Dependence Fortieth Meeting" href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e538">

1616n.d.</a>

1617). Since January 2019, the European Union (EU) has classified CBD as a novel food, implying that before 1997, consumption was insignificant. Each country has implemented the regulation of CBD differently. In the UK, The Food Standards Agency (FSA) recommends limiting the daily dose of CBD to 70mg (Cannabidiol (CBD) <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference n.d." title="Cannabidiol (CBD) . n.d. Food Standards Agency. &#xA; https://www.food.gov.uk/safety-hygiene/cannabidiol-cbd&#xA; &#xA; . Accessed 5 Apr 2020" href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e541">

1618n.d.</a>

1619). However, researchers have used doses up to 1200mg without serious side-effects (Davies and Bhattacharyya <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2019" title="Davies C, Bhattacharyya S. Cannabidiol as a potential treatment for psychosis. Ther Adv Psychopharmacol. 2019;9:204512531988191. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1177/2045125319881916&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e544">

16202019</a>

1621). Conversely, few clinical trials involving children with treatment-resistant epilepsy who received either 10 or 20mg/kg of CBD (Epidiolex) for 12weeks recorded side-effects, such as a reversible rise in liver enzymes (Devinsky et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2018a" title="Devinsky O, Patel AD, Cross JH, Villanueva V, Wirrell EC, Privitera M, et al. Effect of Cannabidiol on drop seizures in the LennoxGastaut syndrome. N Engl J Med. 2018a;378:188897. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1056/NEJMoa1714631&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e547">

16222018a</a>

1623; Thiele et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2018" title="Thiele EA, Marsh ED, French JA, Mazurkiewicz MB, Benbadis SR, Joshi C, et al. Cannabidiol in patients with seizures associated with Lennox-Gastaut syndrome (GWPCARE4): a randomised, double-blind, placebo-controlled phase 3 trial. Lancet. 2018;391:108596. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1016/S0140-6736(18)30136-3&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e550">

16242018</a>

1625).</p>

1626<p>

1627The popularity of CBD can be partly explained by an increasing number of preclinical and clinical studies indicating a range of potential health benefits. However, mass media interest also plays a significant role. Studies suggest CBD might help with mental health symptoms and neurological conditions like experimentally induced anxiety (Zuardi et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1993" title="Zuardi AW, Cosme RA, Graeff FG, Guimaraes FS. Effects of ipsapirone and cannabidiol on human experimental anxiety. J Psychopharmacol. 1993;7:828. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1177/026988119300700112&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e556">

16281993</a>

1629), generalised social anxiety disorder (Bergamaschi et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2011" title="Bergamaschi MM, Queiroz RHC, Chagas MHN, DCG DO, De Martinis BS, Kapczinski F, et al. Cannabidiol reduces the anxiety induced by simulated public speaking in treatment-nave social phobia patients. Neuropsychopharmacology. 2011;36:121926. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1038/npp.2011.6&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e559">

16302011</a>

1631), social phobia (de Faria et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2020" title="de Faria SM, de Morais Fabrcio D, Tumas V, Castro PC, Ponti MA, Hallak JEC, et al. Effects of acute cannabidiol administration on anxiety and tremors induced by a simulated public speaking test in patients with Parkinsons disease. J Psychopharmacol. 2020;34:18996. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1177/0269881119895536&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e562">

16322020</a>

1633), and conditions like PTSD (Elms et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2019" title="Elms L, Shannon S, Hughes S, Lewis N. Cannabidiol in the treatment of post-traumatic stress disorder: a case series. J Altern Complement Med. 2019;25:3927. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1089/acm.2018.0437&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e565">

16342019</a>

1635; Shannon and Opila-Lehman <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2016" title="Shannon S, Opila-Lehman J. Effectiveness of cannabidiol oil for pediatric anxiety and insomnia as part of posttraumatic stress disorder: a case report. Perm J. 2016;20:10811. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.7812/TPP/16-005&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e568">

16362016</a>

1637) schizophrenia (Zuardi et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2006" title="Zuardi AW, Crippa JAS, Hallak JEC, Moreira FA, Guimares FS. Cannabidiol, a Cannabis sativa constituent, as an antipsychotic drug. Braz J Med Biol Res. 2006;39:4219 &#xA; https://doi.org//S0100-879X2006000400001&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e572">

16382006</a>

1639; Leweke et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2012" title="Leweke FM, Piomelli D, Pahlisch F, Muhl D, Gerth CW, Hoyer C, et al. Cannabidiol enhances anandamide signaling and alleviates psychotic symptoms of schizophrenia. Transl Psychiatry. 2012;2:e94. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1038/tp.2012.15&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e575">

16402012</a>

1641; Morgan and Curran <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2008" title="Morgan CJA, Curran HV. Effects of cannabidiol on schizophrenia-like symptoms in people who use cannabis. Br J Psychiatry. 2008;192:3067. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1192/bjp.bp.107.046649&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e578">

16422008</a>

1643; Schubart et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2011" title="Schubart CD, Sommer IEC, van Gastel WA, Goetgebuer RL, Kahn RS, Boks MPM. Cannabis with high cannabidiol content is associated with fewer psychotic experiences. Schizophr Res. 2011;130:21621. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1016/j.schres.2011.04.017&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e581">

16442011</a>

1645), addiction (Hurd et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2019" title="Hurd YL, Spriggs S, Alishayev J, Winkel G, Gurgov K, Kudrich C, et al. Cannabidiol for the reduction of cue-induced craving and anxiety in drug-abstinent individuals with heroin use disorder: a double-blind randomized placebo-controlled trial. Am J Psychiatry. 2019;176:91122. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1176/appi.ajp.2019.18101191&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e584">

16462019</a>

1647; Hindocha et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2018" title="Hindocha C, Freeman TP, Grabski M, Stroud JB, Crudgington H, Davies AC, et al. Cannabidiol reverses attentional bias to cigarette cues in a human experimental model of tobacco withdrawal. Addiction. 2018;113:1696705. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1111/add.14243&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e587">

16482018</a>

1649; Galaj et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2020" title="Galaj E, Bi GH, Yang HJ, Xi ZX. Cannabidiol attenuates the rewarding effects of cocaine in rats by CB2, 5-HT1A and TRPV1 receptor mechanisms. Neuropharmacology. 2020;167:107740. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1016/j.neuropharm.2019.107740&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e591">

16502020</a>

1651), and epilepsy (Devinsky et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Devinsky O, Cross JH, Wright S. Trial of Cannabidiol for drug-resistant seizures in the Dravet syndrome. N Engl J Med. 2017;377:699700. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1056/NEJMc1708349&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e594">

16522017</a>

1653; Devinsky et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2018b" title="Devinsky O, Patel AD, Thiele EA, Wong MH, Appleton R, Harden CL, et al. Randomized, dose-ranging safety trial of cannabidiol in Dravet syndrome. Neurology. 2018b;90:e120411. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1212/WNL.0000000000005254&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e597">

16542018b</a>

1655; Cunha et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1980" title="Cunha JM, Carlini EA, Pereira AE, Ramos OL, Pimentel C, Gagliardi R, et al. Chronic administration of cannabidiol to healthy volunteers and epileptic patients. Pharmacology. 1980;21:17585. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1159/000137430&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e600">

16561980</a>

1657). These mental health disorders are often co-morbid and include other symptoms CBD might help with, e.g. sleep and impaired cognition. There is also data to suggest CBD could help treat neurodegenerative diseases like Alzheimers disease (Watt and Karl <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Watt G, Karl T. In vivo evidence for therapeutic properties of cannabidiol (CBD) for Alzheimers disease. Front Pharmacol. 2017;8(20). &#xA; https://doi.org/10.3389/fphar.2017.00020&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e603">

16582017</a>

1659; Fernndez-Ruiz et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2013" title="Fernndez-Ruiz J, Sagredo O, Pazos MR, Garca C, Pertwee R, Mechoulam R, et al. Cannabidiol for neurodegenerative disorders: important new clinical applications for this phytocannabinoid? Br J Clin Pharmacol. 2013;75:32333. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1365-2125.2012.04341.x&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e606">

16602013</a>

1661; Esposito et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2006" title="Esposito G, De Filippis D, Carnuccio R, Izzo AA, Iuvone T. The marijuana component cannabidiol inhibits -amyloid-induced tau protein hyperphosphorylation through Wnt/-catenin pathway rescue in PC12 cells. J Mol Med. 2006;84:2538. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1007/s00109-005-0025-1&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e610">

16622006</a>

1663), Parkinsons disease (Fernndez-Ruiz et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2013" title="Fernndez-Ruiz J, Sagredo O, Pazos MR, Garca C, Pertwee R, Mechoulam R, et al. Cannabidiol for neurodegenerative disorders: important new clinical applications for this phytocannabinoid? Br J Clin Pharmacol. 2013;75:32333. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1365-2125.2012.04341.x&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e613">

16642013</a>

1665; Garca-Arencibia et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2007" title="Garca-Arencibia M, Gonzlez S, de Lago E, Ramos JA, Mechoulam R, Fernndez-Ruiz J. Evaluation of the neuroprotective effect of cannabinoids in a rat model of Parkinsons disease: importance of antioxidant and cannabinoid receptor-independent properties. Brain Res. 2007;1134:16270. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1016/j.brainres.2006.11.063&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e616">

16662007</a>

1667), and chronic pain conditions including fibromyalgia (Van De Donk et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2019" title="Van De Donk T, Niesters M, Kowal MA, Olofsen E, Dahan A, Van Velzen M. An experimental randomized study on the analgesic effects of pharmaceutical-grade cannabis in chronic pain patients with fibromyalgia. Pain. 2019;160:8609. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1097/j.pain.0000000000001464&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e619">

16682019</a>

1669), either alone or with THC (Rog et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2005" title="Rog DJ, Nurmikko TJ, Friede T, Young CA. Randomized, controlled trial of cannabis-based medicine in central pain in multiple sclerosis. Neurology. 2005;65:8129. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1212/01.wnl.0000176753.45410.8b&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e622">

16702005</a>

1671; Berman et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2004" title="Berman JS, Symonds C, Birch R. Efficacy of two cannabis based medicinal extracts for relief of central neuropathic pain from brachial plexus avulsion: results of a randomised controlled trial. Pain. 2004;112:299306. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1016/j.pain.2004.09.013&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e625">

16722004</a>

1673; Wade et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2003" title="Wade DT, Robson P, House H, Makela P, Aram J. A preliminary controlled study to determine whether whole-plant cannabis extracts can improve intractable neurogenic symptoms. Clin Rehabil. 2003;17:219. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1191/0269215503cr581oa&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e629">

16742003</a>

1675; Svendsen et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2004" title="Svendsen KB, Jensen TS, Bach FW. Does the cannabinoid dronabinol reduce central pain in multiple sclerosis? Randomised double blind placebo controlled crossover trial. BMJ. 2004;329:253. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1136/bmj.38149.566979.AE&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e632">

16762004</a>

1677; Notcutt et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2004" title="Notcutt W, Price M, Miller R, Newport S, Phillips C, Simmons S, et al. Initial experiences with medicinal extracts of cannabis for chronic pain: results from 34 N of 1 studies. Anaesthesia. 2004;59:44052. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1365-2044.2004.03674.x&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e635">

16782004</a>

1679). Additionally, in more than 30 countries, health authorities have approved CBD, under the name Epidiolex, to treat two severe forms of treatment-resistant childhood epilepsy (Dravet and Lennox-Gastaut syndrome) (Devinsky et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2016" title="Devinsky O, Marsh E, Friedman D, Thiele E, Laux L, Sullivan J, et al. Cannabidiol in patients with treatment-resistant epilepsy: an open-label interventional trial. Lancet Neurol. 2016;15:2708. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1016/S1474-4422(15)00379-8&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e638">

16802016</a>

1681; Silvestro et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2019" title="Silvestro S, Mammana S, Cavalli E, Bramanti P, Mazzon E. Use of cannabidiol in the treatment of epilepsy: efficacy and security in clinical trials. Molecules. 2019;24." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e641">

16822019</a>

1683). Sativex, a sublingual spray containing an equal amount of THC and CBD, is also approved to treat multiple sclerosis in more than 30 countries (Keating <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Keating GM. Delta-9-tetrahydrocannabinol/cannabidiol oromucosal spray (Sativex): a review in multiple sclerosis-related spasticity. Drugs. 2017;77:56374. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1007/s40265-017-0720-6&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e644">

16842017</a>

1685).</p>

1686<p>

1687When used in high doses, somnolence is a primary adverse effect (Machado Bergamaschi et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2011" title="Machado Bergamaschi M, Helena Costa Queiroz R, Waldo Zuardi A, de Souza Crippa JA. Safety and side effects of cannabidiol, a Cannabis sativa constituent. Curr Drug Saf. 2011;6:23749. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.2174/157488611798280924&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e650">

16882011</a>

1689). Patients in CBD clinical trials were more likely to experience sedation (OR 4.21, 95% CI 1.1815.01) and somnolence (OR 2.23, 95% CI 1.074.64) in comparison to placebo (Chesney et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2020" title="Chesney E, Oliver D, Green A, Sovi S, Wilson J, Englund A, et al. Adverse effects of cannabidiol: a systematic review and meta-analysis of randomized clinical trials. Neuropsychopharmacology. 2020:18. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1038/s41386-020-0667-2&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e653">

16902020</a>

1691). Despite this preclinical and experimental research, there is a lack of human clinical trials to establish the efficacy and appropriate CBD indications fully. The effective dose for most of the above indications is still to be determined. In much of the research, high doses of CBD are used (between 300 and 1200mg), whilst at the same time, globally, millions of CBD users are using low dose CBD. Thus, a disconnect exists between clinical research and the current state of the market.</p>

1692<p>

1693A cross-sectional study of 2409 cannabidiol users from the USA found that the top three medical conditions reported were chronic pain, arthritis/joint pain, and anxiety, followed by depression and insomnia (Corroon and Phillips <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2018" title="Corroon J, Phillips JA. A cross-sectional study of cannabidiol users. Cannabis Cannabinoid Res. 2018;3:15261. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1089/can.2018.0006&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e660">

16942018</a>

1695). A recent survey carried out by Wheeler et al. of 340 young adults, some of whom were CBD users, found the top reasons to be stress relief, relaxation, and sleep improvement. They found edible CBD products to be the most prevalent (Wheeler et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2020" title="Wheeler M, Merten JW, Gordon BT, Hamadi H. CBD (Cannabidiol) product attitudes, knowledge, and use among young adults. Subst Use Misuse. 2020;55:113845. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1080/10826084.2020.1729201&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e663">

16962020</a>

1697). Another study of 400 CBD patients in New Zealand observed an increase in overall quality of life, a decrease in perceived pain, depression, and anxiety symptoms, as well as an increase in appetite and better sleep (Gulbransen et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2020" title="Gulbransen G, Xu W, Arroll B. Cannabidiol prescription in clinical practice: an audit on the first 400 patients in New Zealand. BJGP Open. 2020;4:bjgpopen20X101010. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.3399/bjgpopen20X101010&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e666">

16982020</a>

1699).</p>

1700<p>

1701A national survey indicated that in the UK, 811% of the adult population had tried CBD by June 2019 (Andrew et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2019" title="Andrew G, Liebling YJ, Saoirse BO. The Centre for Medicinal Cannabis; 2019." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e672">

17022019</a>

1703). Studies of Google searches have shown considerable increases in CBD interest, with 6.4 million unique searchers in the USA in April 2019 (Leas et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2019" title="Leas EC, Nobles AL, Caputi TL, Dredze M, Smith DM, Ayers JW. Trends in internet searches for cannabidiol (CBD) in the United States. JAMA Netw Open. 2019. &#xA; https://doi.org/10.1001/jamanetworkopen.2019.13853&#xA; &#xA; ." href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e675">

17042019</a>

1705). Yet it is clear that scientists, physicians, and governments were not prepared for the rapid surge in CBD use.</p>

1706<p>

1707The regulatory confusion, along with recent media hype, has made it hard for most people to understand the true nature of CBD. Being classified as both a medicine and a supplement in some forms, whilst an illegal substance in others leads to consumer and patient confusion and potential frustration. Therefore, this study aimed to understand users consumption patterns regarding dose, route of administration, and reasons for using CBD. We hypothesised that out of all reasons for using CBD, the top three would be anxiety, sleep disturbances, and stress.</p>

1708</div>

1709</div>

1710</section>

1711<section data-title="Methods">

1712<div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section">

1713<h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">

1714Methods</h2>

1715<div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content">

1716<p>

1717We developed an anonymous online questionnaire to collect CBD users self-reported characteristics, preferred method/s, and reason/s for using CBD. The survey was hosted on Survey Monkey Inc. (San Mateo, CA, USA). Data was collected between 10 January 2020 and 18 March 2020. The 20 questions were designed as multiple-choice questions with the option to choose either one or more answers. For some questions, respondents could write an alternative response if no option matched. We collected demographic information (age, sex, and location), CBD use patterns, reasons for use, other medication use, perceived effects, and side effects. The full questionnaire is provided in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#MOESM1">

1718supplementary materials</a>

1719.</p>

1720<p>

1721To access actual CBD users, we collaborated with four different CBD brands and retailers (TheDrug.Store, OTO CBD, With Pollen and Grass &amp; Co.), based in the UK, who sent out the survey to their email databases. The survey was sent out to 14,743 unique email addresses. Two thousand five hundred thirty-four were opened and 475 clicked through to the survey. We also shared the survey with CBD user groups on social media channels like Facebook and LinkedIn. We did not collect any personal data or IP addresses. Ethical approval was not required since this research investigated non-sensitive information using anonymous survey procedures with participants not defined as vulnerable. In addition, participation was deemed unlikely to induce undue psychological stress or anxiety based on ethics committee guidelines (UCL REC <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference n.d." title="UCL REC. n.d. &#xA; https://ethics.grad.ucl.ac.uk/exemptions.php&#xA; &#xA; . Accessed 30 Apr 2020" href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d102082430e695">

1722n.d.</a>

1723).</p>

1724<h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">

1725Statistical analysis</h3>

1726<p>

1727All analyses were conducted in SPSS version 24 (IBM Corporation, Armonk, NY). Valid percentages are reported rather than absolute values for descriptive statistics to account for missing data. We only report data on those reporting using CBD themselves equivalent to 90% of the respondents (e.g., not for veterinary use, not those who had not tried it, and those reporting on behalf of other users). An analysis of non-responders can be found in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#MOESM1">

1728supplementary materials</a>

1729. We conducted logistic regression models to investigate associations between sex (males [reference category] and females), age (recoded to &lt;34years old [reference category], between 35 and 54 years old, and 55+) and location (UK [reference category], other). For CBD use patterns, we used separate models to compare those who did and did not report their primary use of CBD for self-perceived anxiety, stress, and sleep whilst controlling for sex, age, and location. We dummy-coded time of day as each category versus all others. We report adjusted odds ratios with 95% confidence intervals and <i>

1730p</i>

1731 values with a defined cut-off of 0.05.</p>

1732</div>

1733</div>

1734</section>

1735<section data-title="Results">

1736<div class="c-article-section" id="Sec4-section">

1737<h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec4">

1738Results</h2>

1739<div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec4-content">

1740<p>

1741The most significant findings were that many CBD users reported that CBD could improve sleep problems, stress, and anxiety and be used for general health and wellbeing. In the detailed results below, you can find the demographics of our survey population (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#Tab1">

17421</a>

1743), the CBD use patterns (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#Tab2">

17442</a>

1745), and logistic regression and ORs for the different subgroups. The indications for CBD use are shown (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#Fig1">

17461</a>

1747), as well as how CBD affects sleep (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#Fig2">

17482</a>

1749), and other effects of CBD (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/s42238-021-00061-5#Fig3">

17503</a>

1751). Using CBD for sleep was associated with taking it in the evening, and using CBD for anxiety or stress was associated with the sublingual route. Females had higher odds of using CBD for anxiety and men for post-workout. Details of the results can be found below.

1752</p>
